At first, his girl-friend Lisa Fremont (Grace Kelly) and his home-help nurse Stella (Thelma Ritter) think he's imagining it - just bored by his inactivity. B008AS5UAO|5.0|Whoever masterminded this recording has taken the task seriously. It's been a long time since Don Giovanni was accorded a cast entirely filled by major vocal stars. The most recent Dons have tended to be period performances (e.g., from Gardiner, Harding, and Jacobs), with the emphasis on dramatic vitality over stellar singing, although the three versions I've cited have very good singing. Here there seems to be a conscious effort to return to the era of Giulini's classic EMI set, which assembled the best singers for each part that the great European opera houses could deliver. On this new Don the stars may not be household names, but it would be hard to argue that Europe - and in one case, America - could provide a better cast.<br /><br />The overture acquaints us with the prevailing style, with vibratoless strings and light, period-influenced textures. Nezet-Seguin's strong suit, I think, is opera, and he manages his forces with confidence and energy. He often takes measured tempos in order to support the inner drama of the characters. HIPness only tinges this performance; it is recognizably a modern-day rendition, not an \\"authentic\\" one. First up in one of the best singers that the general public doesn't really know, Italian baritone Luca Pisaroni, who has a strong voice, fine musicality, and dramatic presence. It's unusual to find a Leporello whose voice is so much lighter in color than the don's, yet in the modern vein, this peasant sidekick isn't played for comedy so much as lower class resentment, so the comic effect isn't present.<br /><br />DG has placed its faith - and considerable risk - in Ildebrando D'Arcangelo, having released a solo disc of Mozart arias preceding this Don G. The voice is dark and suave, a caffe mocha without milk, and D'Arcangelo's ace in the hole, besides a strong stage presence, is that he's capable of sounding sleek and seductive. I can't think of a basso Don who has exuded such vocal command since the days of Cesare Siepi. Hearing unusual vocal colors continues with the choice of Diana Damrau as Donna Anna, a role mostly assigned to dramatic sopranos - it was even undertaken by Birgit Nilsson - and rarely, in my experience, to a coloratura soprano, although there is the category of dramatic coloratura. Damrau, who sings the Queen of the Night, has come to fit Donna Anna's deeper, weightier range. Even better, she flashes and sparkles in a role that often becomes a bore.<br /><br />Pausing here for the moment, since these are the singers we meet in the first scene, the promise of starry casting is certainly fulfilled - every number proceeds with such dramatic confidence and (mostly) ease of production that you cannot help but be impressed. In addition, this is a real performance of the opera, despite deriving from the concert hall. As the string of stars continues to unfold - Villazon as Don Ottavio, Mojca Erdmann as Zerlina, Joyce Didonato as Donna Elvira - the ambitions of an all-star Don Giovanni are beautifully fulfilled. Even Masetto sounds like he could step up and do quite a respectable Don himself.<br /><br />As much as I've focused on the vocalism, and as superb as it is, what really impresses here is every singer's ability to act with the voice. In \\"Dalla sua pace\\" Villazon is far from the usual lyric tenor, and sticklers may detect some vocal strain and a bit of lunging (also true with Damrau), but how well Villazon turns a cardboard figure into a man in love with real feeling for his lover's distress. The human dimension holds true form top to bottom in the cast. Deficits? Only the lack of humor, which is deliberate in a performance that eschews stagy winks and nods. This is far more \\"dramma\\" than \\"gicoso.\\"<br /><br />In all, this is the closest to a classic Don Giovanni on the grand scale (with a few nods to musical fashion) that we've had since the heyday of Giulini, or perhaps going back as far as Furtwangler. I am deeply impressed.<br /><br />Ildebrando D'Arcangelo (Don Giovanni), Luca Pisaroni (Leporello), Diana Damrau (Donna Anna), Joyce DiDonato (Donna Elvira), Rolando Villaz&oacute;n (Don Ottavio), Mojca Erdmann (Zerlina), Konstantin Wolff (Masetto), Vitalij Kowaljow (Il Commendatore)<br /><br />Mahler Chamber Orchestra, Yannick N&eacute;zet-S&eacute;guini), Luca Pisaroni (Leporello), Diana Damrau (Donna Anna), Joyce DiDonato (Donna Elvira), Rolando Villaz&oacute;n (Don Ottavio), Mojca Erdmann (Zerlina), Konstantin Wolff (Masetto), Vitalij Kowaljow (Il Commendatore)<br /><br />Mahler Chamber Orchestra, Yannick N&eacute;zet-S&eacute;guin

Mac users should think of it being like the Finder on the Mac OS and OS X operating systems, which is responsible for the overall user-management of files and such. If you've never used the Windows Explorer on your PC or the Finder on your Mac, then in all honesty you might find this app to be of little use.<br /><br />Speaking subjectively, I had downloaded this app in its earlier version here for the primary purpose of easily finding files on my Kindle Fire, and copying PDF files to the 'Documents' directory. 0380789035|3.0|Here is what happened to me while reading the book, which is quite telling of some of its problems: I was reading it on my Kindle and had just come to a new chapter. I took a break and came back later to read. I inadvertently skipped a few chapters forward in the story. I read for a good 30-45 minutes until reaching something in the story that made me say \\"wait a minute.\\"<br /><br />And thus is Gaiman's presentation in this book: episodic, choppy, sporadic. We are dealing with gods here folks! Anything can happen. People will randomly appear, supernatural events will occurs, there will be dream-like sequences, flash backs, fast travel, disappearances, slight of hand. You've got it all. Jump a few chapters ahead and you might not notice. The story doesn't fit well in a sequence, so as a reader, it was hard to put all the pieces into place.<br /><br />The book's basis is solid. Shadow becomes the right-hand man of an old god named Odin who is a con man looking to be restored to old glory. Along the way, they meet gods of all different mythologies that were brought to America in hopes of recruiting them for a final battle against America's new gods of media and internet in a final showdown. Gaiman's depiction of small town America littered with all these gods of ancient origins is supremely well done.<br /><br />But along with it's sporadic storyline, another thing happened that deflated this supposed epic: the presentation of the new gods. The story is really one sided. Gaiman spent a good while on wonderful characterizations of ancient gods and Americanized them in very believable ways. The New Gods don't get that kind of treatment; they make their appearances as the \\"we are the bad guys.\\" Think about the fertile ground there was for that, how internet, media, gluttony and all we praise today could have been characterized.<br /><br />So why the three stars? Because even with those flaws, the concept was so solid and the ending of the book ties all of these lose strings together in a satisfactory way. Don't be surprised if this turns into a classic page turner for you: the old American Gods can be quite interesting that you want to continue to find more. The presentation wasn't my favorite, but Gaiman still has created a story that resonated with me.gs together in a satisfactory way. The presentation wasn't my favorite, but Gaiman still has created a story that resonated with me.

B001IMFHVG|5.0|This incredible recital which took place in The Great Hall of the  Moscow Conservatory in 1962, has been digitally remastered for this DVD and although the picture is black and white, the sound is quite good evoking comparisons with RCA's \\"Living Stereo\\" series.Rubinsteins recordings usually took place in the studio, but if you believe aside from being a superb musician, that his playing is modest and conservative, guess again.<br /><br />This live recital catches Rubinstein in a totally inspired mood, giving virtuoso performances that sound straight from the 19th cenury comparable to such greats as Josef Lhevinne, Josef Hofmann, Moritz Rosenthal, Leopold Godowsky,young Horowitz and Rachmaninoff. The Polonaise in F# minor is dazzling, but its the 2nd Piano Sonata where Rubinstein gives every note meaning combined with superb virtuosity that is always at the service of the composer, a case in point being the 2nd movement (Scherzo) where you can hear all the double thirds and octaves clearly and distinctly, at least equal to such greats as Horowitz,  Argerich and Gilels.<br /><br />These are risk taking performances much more free than the Rubinstein we know in the studio.The Chopin Etudes (4), Barcarolle, and Waltz in A minor show Rubinstein at the peak of his virtuoso and musical capabilities, and the Polonaise in A flat major \\"Heroic\\" will never be surpassed.The power, feeling, expression and complete identification with every note prove that Artur Rubinstein is a 19th century virtuoso at heart with every note of Chopin's music in his bones.A historic document and a desert island must have.

6305076995|4.0|Recently I have been having a bit of a personal YES revival. I'm currently reading Bruford's excellent book ([[ASIN:1906002231 Bill Bruford The Autobiography]]), just watched [[ASIN:B001BWYT9Y Yes: The Directors Cut]] from their 2003 reunion tour, and even got [[ASIN:B00007LTIC Going for the One]], never one of my favorites, but the one I saw them tour for in the fall of '77. And I am happy to say that of the music, this DVD from '72 is the best yet. Sure the sound is muddy and of questionable pitch - actually it's terrible, but it's not much different than being in the kind of acoustically challenged halls they often had to play in, and if you want the best sound, just listen to the CDs. Instead consider it a bonus, all the easier to imagine actually being there and being really stoned so you could care less (which is pretty much how the audience looks).<br /><br />And what really matters here is seeing the band. Compared to the 2003 show, which is pretty amazing as well, if slightly mellower and more mature, this early show (an abbreviated 70 minute performance from December 1972 at the Rainbow Theatre, London) shows them at their peak - at least creatively, if not personally and professionally - a mix of youthful hubris, and musical virtuosity; visionary, confident, showy and enthusiastic. As different as they all are, they all appear to be playing their hearts out. Howe is especially skilled and animated - frankly amazing, and I feel foolish to have never noticed just how much (and how well) he plays the slide guitar. And Anderson, although doing nothing much more than standing there singing, provides the glue that holds it all together. Then as now I have never been able to figure out what he is singing about, but I have never really thought it important to try. Instead I listen to the music of how he says it, letting a few key words register and take my mind with them like the abstract bits of poetry they are. And Wakeman's sequined cape is pretty cool, not to mention his array of analog instruments, and his 6 Wives solo medley is pure over-the-top keyboard wizardry that is... just so 70's. And Squire has some kind of bat wings outfit on..., while White just looks so happy to be playing!<br /><br />Mostly though, this is an unpretentious, even low budget, DVD - no quality audio options, or fancy camera work, and even the concert has minimal stage lights/effects (there is a mirror ball and projected images of microscopic pond life though...). But the performance is great and the video work is adequate to show them doing it. For me, the highlight is seeing the teaser 3 minute glimpse of \\"Starship Trooper\\" during the closing credits. For the dedicated YES fan this is a 5 star must, especially if you weren't around for this era (focuses mostly on Fragile and Close to the Edge). For those more casually interested in the music, it's more like 3 star, and I recommend just spending your money on the remastered albums from the early 70's.<br /><br />Perhaps for me the best part is seeing, from this perspective of 40 years later, just how ambitious the music was, and how that's what they were into without much extra frills and fluff. B005LAII8A|5.0|It is absolutely glorious when intelligent sci-fi not only re-emerges, but returns and flourishes with spectacular results. Tucked neatly into this technologically and sometimes extraterrestrial genre of film is time travel. While time travel films aren't always done right, when they are they make one hell of an impact; the \\"Back to the Future\\" films, the \\"Terminator\\" films, \\"Donnie Darko,\\" \\"Time Bandits,\\" \\"Twelve Monkeys,\\" \\"Source Code,\\" and the fantastic little Spanish film \\"Timecrimes\\" are just a few examples. Combine that potential with the impressive writer/director Rian Johnson and an incredible cast that includes Joseph Gordon-Levitt, Bruce Willis, and Emily Blunt and you have one of the most anticipated films of the year on your hands with \\"Looper.\\"<br /><br />In the year 2044, time travel has not yet been invented but 30 years from now it will have been. After being labeled as illegal, the ability to leap through time is only available on the black market. People who criminal organizations want to get rid of are sent back to 2044 where hired guns called \\"loopers\\" dispose of them. Loopers make very good money and Joe (Joseph Gordon-Levitt) is living the good life. When a looper's contract nears its expiration, the mob sends back their future self who they then get rid of and have the next 30 years to live their life. It's called \\"closing the loop.\\" But when Joe's future self (Bruce Willis) is sent back, his life is turned upside down.<br /><br />While you may not be able to totally buy that Joseph Gordon-Levitt is a younger version of Bruce Willis based on looks alone (even with the use of prosthetics), Gordon-Levitt does deserve credit for developing the ability to act like Willis along with similar facial expressions, mannerisms, and body language. Bruce Willis' character is a really interesting piece of the puzzle. The older version of Joe is committing some of the most heinous crimes imaginable, but his reasoning for it is just. As you're shown his back story, you understand what he's going through and almost find yourself rooting for him. He's like a villain with heart.<br /><br />Emily Blunt is really fantastic. This might be the most straightforward and emotional that she has ever been in any performance. The addition of her son Cid (Pierce Gagnon) may make or break the film for you as it goes down a path that may find you comparing it to other films. The Cid character is an intriguing one though as his emotions are out of control, but his intentions are good and he's fairly amusing more often than not. While Paul Dano doesn't get a whole lot of screen time, it's amazing that that underhanded smarminess he's often associated with is still able to shine through.<br /><br />This is Rian Johnson's best work by a landslide and that's coming from someone who absolutely loves his first two films. While the concept is somewhat familiar, it's always going in places you don't expect them to go. Seth's (Paul Dano) side plot is around the time your jaw will hit the floor. The ending is really magnificent, as well. It feels tragic on one hand, but extremely fitting on the other. While the soundtrack seems to kind of pick and choose its moments to really stand out, there are a few instances where it's emotionally overwhelming. It will only get better in repeat viewings and listens.<br /><br />\\"Looper\\" is an astounding piece of cinema no matter how you slice it. Featuring fantastic performances from the entire cast, absorbing cinematography, and one of the most unique concepts for a film in quite some time, \\"Looper\\" is one of the most creative, intelligent, and unpredictable films of the year.story, you understand what he's going through and almost find yourself rooting for him. After cloistering myself in the airport bathroom and crying for 40 minutes, curled up on the floor in fetal position, bemoaning the fact that we are exporting our illiterate fads to nations near and far, I dusted myself off and returned to the bookstore. B004EPYZXK|5.0|One of the more unusual entries in the found-footage field, Apoloo 18 pieces together footage of a secret final mission to the moon in the 1970s, launched at night under cover of a routine satellite launch.  Using tapes and film reels from before the mission launch on Earth as well as material recorded onboard the moon lander, the orbiting ship and even cameras and microphones on and in the astronauts's space suits, the movie follows the events on that fateful mission and reveals the meaning behind the film's tagline, \\"There's a reason we've never been back to the moon.\\"<br /><br />The small crew believes they're being sent to the moon to set up equipment that will serve as part of a space-based early warning system against a potential Russian missile attack.  After setting down, the astronauts make the surprise discovery of a Russian craft already in the vicinity, apparantly a recent landing, hastily abandoned and with no sign of the cosmonauts themselves.  From there, they go from simply making surprise discoveries to suspecting that there's A Lot about this mission that the higher-ups never told them, as findings and unexplained happenings on the moon's surface go from the merely unexpected to the baffling and eerie.<br /><br />Sometimes a movie rises and falls on how well it can make the really 'out there' seem real and credible.  If one was to just write down some of what happens in the movie's final stages it might sound preposterous to someone who hasn't seen the movie, but here the filmmakers make it work.  Well, to me they certainly did. B002SAS26E|5.0|I've gotten into the hobby of game collecting (settlers of catan, ticket to ride, pandemic, dixit, etc.). The internet recommended this nice 2 player game, and it did not disappoint.<br /><br />Jaipur is a pretty intense game of trading and selling of goods as you and your opponent collect goods from the market (table). What makes this trading game particularly interesting are the camels which serve as proxy cards with no trade value, except at the end, yet help or disrupt the collection of cards coming into the market.<br /><br />The design of this game fascinates me. Setup is easy with a few stacks of tokens and a single deck of cards making one shared table space. Its rules are clear, short, and easy to pick up. However, despite its concise rules and setup, it has a surprising amount of depth with tense chance and strategy coming at you with each turn:<br /><br />- Trade item now? Or wait for the market to bring more of the same good and trade in for a bonus? Be mindful of the precious points lost when not making the first trade for that good! No more than 7 cards in that hand!<br /><br />- What about those camel cards in the market? Do you collect them all (no more, no less) to help collect more cards in the future (camels don't count against the hand count)? Or do you wait and have your opponent collect them and open up the market for fresh (potentially nice) cards.<br /><br />- What?! The game is finished already?! (Opponent cleared third stock of goods or deck is depleted [game over!])<br /><br />Game time ranges from ~20-30 minutes to play best of 3 which the game is set up to play for. It's fast and can be over before you know it.<br /><br />It'll take a couple of rounds to get a solid feel for the game.<br /><br />A bit pricey for the amount of stuff in the box (cards, tokens, and instructions in english, french, and german). But all the contents is of very good quality with nice durable cards and thick, stiff, cardboard token pieces. The box is of nice quality cardboard and stores the cards and tokens on a good quality plastic tray. Most importantly, its gameplay is unique, innovative, brutal, and concise. Overall, I'm satisfied with the purchase and feel this game has some mileage ahead.<br /><br />Easy to travel with, recommend a small, stable table space of sorts for laying down cards.<br /><br />Highly recommended.ality cardboard and stores the cards and tokens on a good quality plastic tray. B005LAII8A|2.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />The near universal critical acclaim for 'Looper' is something I'm having a hard time fathoming. I suppose we haven't seen a great sci-fi thriller in such a long time, that as soon as a mediocre to average flick such as this one comes along, everyone is jumping on the bandwagon, practically calling it a masterpiece!<br /><br />My first problem with 'Looper' is the overall concept. A crime syndicate in the future (2074 )wants to dispose of its victims by sending them 30 years back into the past and have them disposed of by hit men, who they pay with bars of silver. Once these hit men (the loopers) live their lives for thirty years, the syndicate decides to dispose of them by sending them back to 2044, where they'll be disposed of, sometimes even by their younger selves.<br /><br />It's explained that in the future bodies are 'tagged' so by sending them back in time, they cannot be traced. But why can't the syndicate send their victims in the present immediately back to 65 million B.C. where they would probably be immediately eaten by a dinosaur or other prehistoric animal, thus preventing the time-line from being tampered with? And by cutting out the middlemen (i.e. the loopers) in this way, the syndicate can keep all the profits for themselves.<br /><br />Once we're introduced to the novelty of the how the looper scheme operates, the novelty soon wears off. We see Joe (Joseph Gordon-Levitt) traveling to the cornfield and then performing executions one too many times. Finally, there's a plot twist: fellow looper Seth appears at Joe's apartment, explaining that his 30 year old self from the future appears in the cornfield and he's unable to finish the job. Abe, the enforcer from the future, forces Joe to betray Seth, who's tortured and his alter ego from the future is hunted down. You might ask what this has to do with time travel or science-fiction. Actually nothing--it's simply all action-thriller at this point in time in the script.<br /><br />Soon, Joe finds himself in the same position as Seth. His 30 year old future self (Bruce Willis) appears but manages to prevent younger Joe from doing him in. In a rather derivative idea borrowed from the 'Terminator' series, Willis must dispose of a child who will one day grow up to be the 'Rainmaker', the sinister figure in the future who is now bent on eliminating all the loopers.<br /><br />It might have been interesting had we seen what this Rainmaker character looked and acted like in the future, but that never occurs. We're asked to be content with meeting little Cid, our future Rainmaker, who has telekinetic powers coupled with a severe anger management problem. One is immediately reminded of Billy Mumy in the Season 3 Twilight Zone episode, \\"It's a Good Life\\", who plays a mutant who terrifies adults by 'wishing them away to the cornfield'. So the second half of 'Looper' feels much more like fantasy-horror vs. Sci-fi action thriller.<br /><br />The meeting between the younger and older Joes in the diner is perhaps the most interesting scene in the film as it underscores the contrast between the more impulsive younger self with the more seasoned, experienced older one. Unfortunately, there is hardly any additional interaction between the two altar egos, with older Joe stalking off to blow away Abe and his crew of malevolent meanies. In true Bruce Willis fashion, the aging 'Die Hard' legend, is up to his old tricks, by machine-gunning most of the bad guys, sans Kid Blue, who appears out of nowhere on his flying motorcycle and immediately gets himself killed, instead of Joe, who is his intended target.<br /><br />If the Looper second half feels a bit slower than the first, that's because director Rian Johnson spends a little too much time out on the farm, with Joe courting Sara (Emily Blunt). Although there's a feeling that we've seen this before, the ending proves to be passable as we finally have something to feel good about. Younger Joe sacrifices himself, preventing older Joe from killing Sara. He does this after he's able to foresee that Cid, growing up as the Rainmaker, will hold a perpetual grudge due to the murder of his mother and will take it out on all of humanity (including all the loopers) in the future.<br /><br />Unfortunately, Joe's sacrifice, is not enough to make either young Joe or older Joe, likable. Younger Joe, before he takes his own life, is a criminal and a drug addict and older Joe, is responsible for murdering the second child on his list of three potential Rainmakers. In terms of a sympathetic protagonists, there's little to like here at all.<br /><br />'Looper' is also unable to score points with its look at the dystopian future. One feels that one is simply viewing a second hand set from 'Robocop'--in that film, 'Old Detroit' looks remarkably similar to Looper's Kansas City of 2044 including the grimy 'futuristic' cars and emphasis on vagrancy as a national blight.<br /><br />The performances in Looper are all acceptable, with an honorable mention going to little Pierce Gagnon as Cid, who manages to handle a number of complicated lines and take direction in a fashion way above his chronological age.<br /><br />In the end, Looper proves only mildly interesting. While some of the action sequences are entertaining, the sci-fi premise is not developed into something clever enough to deserve all the the accolades that have been heaped upon it to date.nger Joe sacrifices himself, preventing older Joe from killing Sara. 1476712042|3.0|What we have here is a perfect example of synecdoche, in which the title tells you everything you need to know about the book itself.<br /><br />Abby Abernathy wants you to think she is a Good Girl. She adorns herself with preppy cardigans, sticks close to her best friend, and avoids Bad Boys. When she winds up in an underground fight club-type place, she locks eyes with the victorious fighter, Travis Maddox. She is intrigued.<br /><br />The cousin of her best friend's boyfriend (I know, I had the same eye roll), Travis is straight out of Central Casting for \\"Bad Boys,\\" down to the tattoos and the penchant for one night stands. He never met an emotional attachment that he cared to make, other than with his family. And when he sees Abby, he is intrigued.<br /><br />Abby's first move is brilliant: she acts completely unimpressed. And she is, to some degree. Yes, she is attracted to him, but she refuses to be another notch on his scarred belt. She dresses down, she is rude and sarcastic, and she tells him that he has no chance with her. When they spend the night together, they actually SLEEP together. No hanky panky occurs. Travis, for all of his overflowing testosterone, likes Abby; he genuinely enjoys her company and is determined to not mess up this friendship.<br /><br />We know, however, that he will mess it up, and Abby will as well. It is just a matter of pages until they wind up romantically linked, especially when Abby loses a bet and has to move in with Travis for a month. What is good about their first encounter is that Abby makes the decision to be with him. Travis empowers her in that sense, and it's nice to read some YA lit that does not have the girl swooning uncontrollably over her hard-to-read love interest.<br /><br />And that's where the \\"disaster\\" portion of this book comes into play. Travis! What in the world happens to you? What fresh hell does Jamie McGuire do to you?<br /><br />Here is one answer: she takes a strong, interesting character and turns him into one that is veritably emasculated, dull, and insipid. Travis becomes the girl; he cries and screams and begs. He ditches his boxes of condoms along with his dignity, and we lose interest in him just as quickly. The man who oozed masculinity and dominance now simpers along, weak and uninspired. We are meant to see that Abby changed him from Bad Boy to Boy Worth Loving, but instead he comes across as utterly unappealing.<br /><br />Don't even get me started on the ending. Not only does it defy all logic, it appears to defy its characters. We are led to believe certain things about Abby and Travis, yet McGuire ends in it a way that is so unfaithful to them that you wonder if there was a publishing error in which another book's ending was cut and pasted onto this one.<br /><br />The first half of this book is 'beautiful.' Abby, Travis and their friends are created with care and creativity. We like them. We are interested in them. We care about them. And then comes the 'disaster,' and you will want to shout in frustration.<br /><br />This could have been so good. But it just is not.<br /><br />One word of warning: while this is Young Adult lit, the emphasis is on \\"adult.\\" There are fairly detailed sexual situations, as well as alcohol usage.<br /><br />Published on cupcake's book cupboard. @VivaAmaRisata<br />Thanks to NetGalley for the preview.rns him into one that is veritably emasculated, dull, and insipid. B005LAII8A|5.0|Should we anthropomorphize the film INCEPTION the first thing it might well say is: \\"LOOPER is the most intelligent and ba11sy sci-fi picture since, well, me.\\" And maybe it'll even observe: \\"Doesn't Leonardo diCaprio hava a humongous head disproportionate to his body?\\" (But that's probably me projecting.) I love time travel stories almost more than I love my dog. And if these same stories should come with the clever and the inventiveness and the sheer pizazz, then I love them more than my nana (who hates my dog). LOOPER thrills and provokes thought and, with its dizzying twists, compels you to stay alert. But perhaps the most remarkable bit is that while director/writer Rian Johnson demonstrates great flair in establishing the dystopian, near futuristic backdrop and, with it, the concept of time travel, he switches things up and grips you with character work. I wasn't expecting to be so drawn into the characters. LOOPER presents a cast in its finest form.<br /><br />The year is 2044, and near-future Kansas has assumed a BLADERUNNERy urban aesthetic, if not exactly in visuals then at least in slick-grungy bleakness. It's a world in which 10% of the population has mutated into low-level telekinetics, able only to float a quarter, maybe make it spin around in the air if they're extra-gifted. The TKs are regarded as jokes, as walking social gaffes, but never mind. The head-boggling stuff launches with this premise: Thirty years from 2044, time travel will be invented but then promptly declared illegal. Except when did crooks ever toe the line? In the future's future, crime syndicates apply time travel to dispose of their dirty laundry, of their unfinished business. Victims targeted for execution are sent back to the past, cuffed and bagged over their head, bar of silver affixed upon their person. Back in 2044 contracted trigger men called \\"loopers\\" arrive at an assigned place and time. When the victim manifests from thin air, the looper mows him down with a primitive blunderbuss. The silver is payment for services rendered. And Joe Simmons (Gordon-Levitt), ice water and apathy and strange pharmaceuticals running in his veins, has amassed quite a pile of silver.<br /><br />When the crime syndicate wants to terminate a looper's contract, it sends the older version of that looper thirty years back in time, gold bars strapped to him, to be killed by his younger self, and this is called \\"closing the loop.\\" The gold is the younger self's retirement fee. So imagine the existential angst that comes with knowing that you've murdered yourself and that your time on earth has a known expiration date. Every looper dreads the coming day when he'll have to eliminate himself. And when Joe Simmons at last meets his older self, he hesitates just long enough for Old Joe to escape. The fit's now hit the shan.<br /><br />From the moment Old Joe (Willis) appears, we're treated to his perspective as well. And it's mostly thru Old Joe's eyes that we learn of the Rainmaker, a shadowy crime lord in 2074 who's bent on eradicating all existing loopers, on closing all the loops. Rian Johnson layers in another plot thread that features a surprisingly tough Emily Blunt playing a weathered (yet very sexy) farm woman named Sara. Joe Simmons, the younger version, is now on the run from the mob because of his bungle. And even though Joe means to hunt down his older self and snuff him and so get back into the mob's good graces, he wanders into Sara's neck of the woods. Interesting stuff happens at the farm.<br /><br />This is the best I've seen Bruce Willis in a while. Shows you how good he can be when he commits fully to a role. He refrains from exhibiting his patented swagger, his showy mannerisms. Going in, I had anticipated siding with Willis' character but I was startled to realize that I was rooting more for Gordon-Levitt's more stoic Joe. Old Joe remains sympathetic as he finds himself faced with having to make a terrible decision. Gordon-Levitt turns in another terrific performance. Grafted with a prosthetic nose and having screwed contacts in, he still only vaguely resembles Willis. But there's enough there in terms of familiar cadence and facial expressions that you don't mind going along with the intent. I can't not mention how very good Jeff Daniels is here. He plays a mobster sent from the future to preside over the syndicate's interests in 2044. Daniels steals more than his share of scenes, his facade of scruffy benevolence masking a sadistic, vindictive streak. Meanwhile, child actor Pierce Gagnon fleshes out Sara's troubled (and, frankly, kinda creepy) 10-year-old son Cid. Gagnon possesses a knack for putting on these terrifying scowls, and there were moments onscreen in which I half-expected Cid to whisk someone to the cornfield, if you get the Bill Mumy reference.<br /><br />LOOPER is no sci-fi for dummies. It's a haunting high concept sci-fi thriller. It's got big brains and an audaciousness and unexpected emotional heft. It offers memorable scenes like the old looper's meeting his very grotesque end and the one in which Sara's ten-year-old exercises an apocalyptic tantrum. But my favorite scene is probably the diner conversation between Young and Old Joe, two distinct people divided by the measure of a dining table and thirty years of added living. LOOPER promotes debate. It dusts off old questions such as \\"If you go back in time and come in contact with your younger self, will both of you implode?\\" (no, you don't) or \\"If you could, would you go back in time and kill H!tler when he was a baby?\\" (...)her terrific performance. B008FIHUDO|5.0|Bettye LaVette is a force of nature. That she is still alive and kicking hard after 50 years of slogging around the peripheries of show business is a testament to the power of this extraordinary musician.  The story of her success, which continues to grow at the age of 66 (born January 29, 1946), is bittersweet and heartwarming.  Her bio can be found in many places and she's been very busy giving interviews in support of this new CD, so I'll simply focus on the music at hand.<br /><br />I was lucky enough to hear her perform most of this CD live before I picked it up at the release party in New York City. I'm still buzzing from the experience.  The song selection is brilliant here, with every cut reflecting some aspect of her life experience, personality or philosophy.  Bettye is quoted as saying that she doesn't care who wrote a song or who sang it:  once she sings it, it's an R&B song and it belongs to HER.  Indeed, it does.<br /><br />Bettye is a ferociously intelligent singer whose commitment to the meaning of a song is absolute.  Combine that commitment with one of the funkiest and most expressive R&B voices ever, and the sterling contribution of her band, who serve Bettye in the manner she deserves, and you've got the makings of high art.  Her mission seems to be to strive for an authenticity, honesty, self-reflection and unself-righteous sermonizing that most performers can't touch.  Listen to her renditions of Dylan's \\"Everything is Broken,\\" the Gnarls Barkley hit \\"Crazy,\\" Sly Stone's \\"Thankful N' Thoughtful,\\" Beth Nielsen Chapman's \\"Fair Enough,\\" and the remarkable \\"The More I Search The More I Die,\\" and you'll see what I mean.<br /><br />A special highlight is \\"Dirty Old Town,\\" done two ways.  The explanation for this dual presentation is that her producer wanted a funky mid-tempo approach, but Bettye and her band (\\"being from Detroit,\\" as she put it), wanted to do it like a funeral dirge.  In the end, she chose both.  But it's the funeral dirge that winsthe day.  The song, about life in a working class town in Lancashire, England, was written in the 1940's by Ewan MacColl (who also wrote \\"First Time Ever I Saw Your Face\\").  Bettye changed the place names to reflect the areas of Detroit that were important to her as a young girl, and thus paints a deeply personal, heart rending picture of growing up, loving and attempting to thrive in a bleak economic and social ghetto.<br /><br />And anyone who likes Otis Redding and Al Greene will really enjoy her old school take on Patti Griffin's \\"Time Will Do The Talking,\\" which coulda shoulda woulda been a big hit back in the day.<br /><br />\\"Thankful N' Thoughtful\\" is a record of one remarkable artist's survival and triumph through all manner of strife and transcendence.  Bettye is very generous, and if you accept the deep gifts she offers here, you'll be changed.<br /><br />Addendum:  It's a sin that this terrific CD was not nominated for a Grammy this year.

B008I3QZKU|4.0|I haven't purchased an album by The Killers since their first album, Hot Fuss, until now. Yes, there is a some cheesiness to the lyrics but there's great emotion behind them and driving music. I can hear hints of U2 and even James in this album, but I probably get Freddie Mercury (of Queen) coming through the most. A newer band called Broken Records is doing similarly dramatically-melodic songs so if you like The Killers, check them out, or vice-verse -- if you like Broken Records' sound, then this album has similarities. The melodies are soaring, and Brandon Flowers' voice has not deteriorated over time unlike some lead singers who've made several albums and gone on several tours (and all of the drinking and/or smoking that can accompany that).<br /><br />There aren't any clunkers on the album but there are definitely a set of songs that are stronger than others. \\"Runaways\\" is the lead single and has placed on some charts but it's not the strongest song. Those honors go to \\"Miss Atomic Bomb\\" and \\"Here With Me\\", although both have some of those cringe-worthy lyrics. I give Flowers credit for the power and emotion he puts behind his delivery, and it more than makes up for lyrics such as \\"don't want your picture on my cell phone\\" from \\"Here With Me\\" or \\"don't break character...\\" from \\"Be Still\\".<br /><br />The album finished strong with \\"Be Still\\" and \\"Battle Born\\", but there are many delicious hooks along the way. I don't know if is a previous trademark of The Killers, but there are a few nice patterns I noted with the music on this album. First off, many of the songs have overlapping vocals, either to provide contrast to the main melody being sung or to strengthen it vocally. Second, the albums often have a bridge-like outro. That is, there is frequently a strong, soaring finish to the songs that is beyond just the standard of ending with a repeated chorus. All in all, this is one of the better albums of 2012.

B008220DIC|3.0|\\"The Master\\" is beautifully shot and well acted, but it lacks a satisfying climax. The film follows Freddie Quell, a recently discharged WWII vet, as he adapts to civilian life. His unstable personality and stupidity ill-suit him for steady employment, and before long he's on the run for poisoning a co-worker. Now a drifter, he comes across a yacht owned by Lancaster Dodd, a cult leader who takes Freddie in as one of his followers.<br /><br />It is puzzling the way the other characters take to Freddie. He is dim, incoherent and prone to unpredictable fits of rage. And yet, attractive women find him irresistible. He has a brief romance with a gorgeous redhead, makes out with a hot department store employee in a darkroom and gets a busty British chick in the sack merely by gesturing to her. One has to wonder though if Freddie is hallucinating these scenes. Before he is discharged from the military, he is questioned by a superior officer about some psychological issues he had during his service, including a vision of his mother. And there is a later scene where Dodd is singing and dancing in a room full of people, when suddenly all of the women in the room are nude. You never see the women undress; they are just suddenly naked. It is obvious that Freddie suffers from some kind of mental illness, maybe schizophrenia. So I have to wonder if the scenes with him making out with beautiful women weren't also hallucinations. After all, what woman wouldn't consider this guy to be a deranged freak?<br /><br />The nature of Dodd's relationship with Freddie is not clear. They are too unlike to be friends; it is not a student-teacher relationship; it's not exploitative either. Nor is Dodd's cult a surrogate family for Freddie, since no one is really attached to him except for Dodd. At times Dodd seems to use Freddie as a laboratory rat for his therapy techniques, but it is obvious by the end of the film that they have no effect on him. Is this what the film is saying? That Dodd'stherapy doesn't work on genuinely disturbed people, and therefore Scientology (on which Dodd's cult is loosely based) is a fraud? In the end Freddie's experiences seem to add up to nothing; his is an aimless tale, and in that sense it is like Paul Thomas Anderson's other movies. \\"The Master\\" is a finely acted, finely written and beautifully filmed picture, but with no larger message overhanging the plot. Perhaps if Freddie's relationship with Dodd had taught us something, it would have been a great film.

0345444051|4.0|The last time I read this book was when it was assigned to my English class in eighth grade, and it was a pleasure to come back to as an adult and re-experience the same emotions I did back then. This is science fiction about the wonder and awe of discovery, the bittersweetness of letting go of the primitive past, and the ultimate destiny of the human race. It's not a dystopian or cautionary tale, as so much science fiction, but a book about what it means for our species to reach adulthood -- and a sacrifice that that may one day demand of us.<br /><br />The story begins, in classic form, with the visitation of beings from the stars. The Overlords arrive on Cold War-era Earth in immense, silver starships, and immediately establish themselves as vastly superior, but benevolent masters. Yet, they refuse to reveal themselves in person (at least not right away) or explain their ultimate purposes. Here, one might guess, as some characters do, at sinister intentions.<br /><br />But, nothing so crude comes to pass, and Clarke proceeds to a new generation of characters, as the Overlords usher in a new era of peace and worldwide prosperity for the human race. Not to mention a certain amount of ennui and loss of purpose, as mankind finds that most of its traditional problems are solved. Yet, a few people continue to puzzle over the mysteries about the Overlords and chafe against the restrictions they still impose. What are the reasons? Several intrepid explorers begin to find out.<br /><br />The writing is simple and unadorned, and the characters not particularly complex in their construction (not to mention a bit 1950s), but there's a subtle eloquence to the way the story unfolds, each stage in the human race's progress revealing a little more about the fate that must eventually come. And Clarke's writing is still a pleasure to read for its vision, its thoughtful ideas about the forms that different alien races might take, the capabilities of advanced technology, and how human society might continue to function when the primary need is that of avoiding boredom. Though a few assumptions are showing their age (newspapers, radio), much of this 1953 story still speaks to the 21st century. Clarke continues to remind us of how little we know about what's out there in the universe, or how limited our evolution has been compared to what's possible.<br /><br />Read it, if you haven't yet. Or read it again. Childhood's End is one of the works that sets the template for great science fiction, and will likely still contain meaning for new readers in fifty years.

B0042816YK|3.0|The view is beautiful, and it's not just Julia Roberts we're looking at; EPL is likely the best looking movie you'll see all year. Of course it helps to have Italy and Indonesia as exotic backdrops, but the art of cinematography should never be underestimated and it's really first class here; I'd go to Bali in a heartbeat, it looked that good.<br /><br />If Julia Roberts went on a spiritual odyssey it might look a lot like EPL. OK, so we all know she's married with twins and gives every indication that she's happy with her lot, but it's actually not as far fetched a notion as it might seem. Apparently she found something to her liking in India whilst filming and embraced Hinduism on her return to New Mexico. Nice though that is, it's really the main problem with the movie.<br /><br />The first time I heard someone call her Liz, as in Liz Gilbert, the real life protagonist on whose book the screenplay and movie are based, it was really grating. Towards the end it became less noticeable as she disappeared more into the role, but not much. I can't decide if she was miscast, or whether she was simply a necessity given the star wattage needed to make such an internally referenced movie.<br /><br />Whilst the book is a chatty affair, a funny, self regalatory dialogue between reader and writer, a bit like listening in on an extended conversation between girlfriends, the movie usually side steps any effort to visually translate this and resorts to extensive use of voice-over. Perhaps a lesser star would have been more capable of disappearing into the role and carrying the weight of the emotional and spiritual journey. I'm not sure why but Naomi Watts keeps coming into my head as I think about it; from an acting point of view someone like her might been a better choice for the role because she has such a talent for raw emotion and for portraying neurotic and needy.<br /><br />Having read the book a couple of times and got a lot from it, I had high hopes for the movie, but after reading a spate of poor reviews I realized I would have to downgrade my expectations. What really surprised me was how negative some of the reviews were; some reviewers really hated the movie and these were reviewers whose opinions I normally seek out and respect. They viewed Liz Gilbert as selfish and self indulgent in the extreme and whilst they may have a point, I think the problem was that they hadn't read the book. Lacking the time to really delve into the reasons for her journey, the movie portrays her as very self seeking in her treatment of the men she left behind. For this reason I almost felt it would have been better to simply launch her straight into the Italian section of her journey and fill in the missing motivation and back story as necessary.<br /><br />Apparently the director and co-writer Ryan Murphy consulted with Elizabeth Gilbert on the screenplay and as a result was able to include material not in the book, even as he had to leave much out. This led to incidents like one just before the movie ends where Julia (sorry, Liz) breaks off her relationship with her Brazilian amour Felipe because she fears it will undermine everything she has sought during her year of exploration, namely balance. Whether this actually happened seemed beside the point; it seemed true to the spirit of her journey and I wished more liberties had been taken in an attempt to get to the heart of the story. With a distinct lack of such resonant moments the movie usually seemed more interested in outer beauty than in portraying her struggle for inner peace.<br /><br />It's a well accepted truism that books and movies are apples and oranges; the movie of a book has to stand alone as a complete entity in itself and this usually means staying true to the spine of the book but substantially reinventing how that is portrayed in this most visual of mediums. It seemed to me the movie lacked the confidence to do this, probably as it didn't want to offend the book's many fans.<br/><br />Having said all that I'm sure the movie will do well, even if it never expands its audience beyond the 8 million readers who bought the book. But whilst the book led to legions of devoted Liz followers (known by some as Lizbians) and touched a nerve somewhere deep in the psyche of many middle aged women seeking reinvention, the movie leaves its audience with nothing of substance. It was probably too much to hope that it would, but it's sad nevertheless that a book that held such meaning for so many should be reduced to nothing more than a 2 hour diversion in a movie theater.

0143038419|5.0|I first came across Elizabeth Gilbert and the phenomenon that is \\"Eat Pray Love\\" a couple of years ago. I read the book and liked it, saw the interview on Oprah but didn't enjoy it as much (it's always a bit disappointing when someone isn't as you imagine them) and then moved on. But I was aware that it had become something huge in its own right, a mega best seller, a work that spoke profoundly to many people.<br /><br />A few months ago I went back to it, read it again and this time made a more leisurely assessment. Her back story is necessary but a bit whiny, Italy is enjoyable if a little superficial, but she really hits her stride in India; I learned a lot about meditation and eastern spirituality in that part, and Indonesia, well it's a great romantic tale the like of which we'd all be seduced by. My conclusion was that the book as a whole was a touchstone for the kind of writing I'm drawn to. I've bored everyone I know with repeated reference to Michael Crichton's \\"Travels\\", but I saw that \\"Eat Pray Love\\" was of similar vein; a very personal account of inner and outer travel, of great adventure, necessary growth and in her case all nicely tied up at the end with unexpected love.<br /><br />Some questions arose though as I thought about EPL, and I did some research to find the answers. I went to Elizabeth Gilbert's website and found some links to interviews that helped to fill in some of the missing pieces.<br /><br />My first question was: why was it so popular? What nerve did it hit with such unerring accuracy? One commentator summed it up well when she said; \\"She tapped into the middle-aged woman culture of self-reinvention ..... and nailed the voice.\\" That seemed very wise to me; why it would speak to so many women, but also why it would speak to me, as if there's one thing I'm all about, its self-reinvention, I mean heck I'm a Californian after all.<br /><br />My second question was; why would getting divorced and finally acknowledging that she didn't wantchildren cause such a cataclysmic meltdown? With some experience of personal trauma myself I felt like something about her story didn't quite add up.<br /><br />I found the answer in two places; in an interview, Gilbert's sister Catherine explained; \\"Liz knew she wanted to be a writer from the time she was 13, but she's so gregarious, a lot of the struggles everyone else goes through in their adolescence and early 20's -- `Who am I? What am I?' -- she managed to avoid.\\"<br /><br />In the introduction to her follow up book \\"Committed\\" Elizabeth Gilbert also makes reference to this; her general indifference to her own well being and unfamiliarity with herself meant that when the tsunami of depression hit around 30 she had no way of understanding or articulating what was happening; her body, then marriage, then mind fell apart.<br /><br />Having read those explanations I understood and felt a lot of compassion. As a result of EPL Elizabeth Gilbert has gained fame and fortune, but she has also taken criticism as being the voice of a very western kind of female mindset, fortunate to have had the kind of problems of an affluent and self involved American woman (and the publishers advance which allowed her to spend a year traveling abroad whilst recovering from them). Whether that is true is not for me to judge, but I do know from personal experience that when your life literally falls apart beneath you and you're left grasping for any point of reference on the way down it can be truly terrifying.<br /><br />I find it interesting that like myself, both Crichton and Gilbert hit big crises around 30. It does make me wonder what it is that can be so difficult about that age, or any age that ends with a zero. I guess like the first week in January there's a wake up call in the offing, an inner directive to face oneself and do the kind of challenging inner work one would never normally have the guts or desire to do.<br /><br />I've been back to both books on occasion, lookingfor something that I never found; what I realized was that it wasn't there in the first place. The books were but a mirror to my inner process, inspiring my own thought and growth. Ultimately I think that is the value of books like these; they provide entertainment on a grand scale and a chance for vicarious living of the best kind, yet they also spark something inside us, remind us we still have desires for adventure and excitement but also to live lives truer to who we really are and take the road less traveled, the road to inner peace and fulfillment.

B0067EKY7M|4.0|Now and then a film comes along with such an 'out of left field' premise, and the producers/director get what it's all about, that it just turns out as the most peasant surprise ever. This is one of those films.<br /><br />Here's the scoop. Ewan McGregor gets an e-mail from Emily Blunt. She has an Arab client with more money than sense who is in love with salmon fishing. He wants McGregor to engineer a system whereby he can introduce salmon into the Yemen ecology. McGregor thinks it's undoable but, since his wife is headed to Geneva on business and Britain needs a public relations coup, (they force the project on him) he might as well see how far he can take it. The results are surprising. There are a couple of sub plots that add to the overall but why spoil?<br /><br />This is a charming film. One of the reasons my wife liked it is because the strongest individuals here are all women. McGregor is very accomplished in his discipline but he's a bit of a milquetoast with little sense of humor. The guy in charge of securing the salmon, and pretty much every male above him in the government, are portrayed as wankers. Emily Blunt is a doll and seeing her and McGregor slowly and quietly grow closer is very satisfying. Something else I liked a lot was that, for the most part, the people here are good-hearted. Even Kristin Scott Thomas, though a bit tough and rough-edged, is not an evil queen. In one scene she's shown playing video games with her son. This is such a jolt, and so quick, it really underlined the type of person she is. There are a lot of nifty touches like that.<br /><br />This is a good looking movie. Even the desert is lovingly photographed. Lasse Hallstrom is a seasoned director and very adept at taking smaller stories and giving them their own gravity. He doesn't disappoint here. You just fall in love with these people and root for them and their cause. The music is good and all the other production values are as good as they need to be to bring this one of a kind film to fruition. If quirky indy type comedies are your poison, this should do you in.

B007L6VR12|2.0|*Spoiler Alert* I just finished watching this film & didn't know at what point to stop watching.  It was was one agonizing scene after the next.  Where do I begin?  Do I start with discussing the emotionless, unrealistic, adolescent acting?  Or analyze the story's unpalatable, schizophrenic love affair?  Clearly, this is two deeply disturbed children that need serious psychiatric evaluation.  I saw one reviewer's title as \\"Stirs the memories of adolescent love\\".  Okay, for one, I personally don't know anybody that has the fond memories of running off into the wilderness at the age of 12-13 with a schizo girlfriend who's capable of stabbing a person.  Nor do I know of any 12 year old's preparing to jump off a church steeple together (Another filmmaker trying to take a blow at the Christians too).  I understand the heart-wrenching crushes we've all endured & my 1st kiss near that age, but this goes far beyond what I can consider a relatable trip down memory lane.  The raw imagery of the girl being felt up by the boy & likewise of the girl describing his erection is far more than the audience needs in trying to captivate the emotions that are being felt.  We're all adults.  We get it or we've been there before!  It borders voyeurism & pedophilia disguised in the name of \\"art\\".  I'm so sick of these masks used in the world of art & film.  Anderson tries to cleverly wrap all of this with his usual bland, retro-colored imagery as recognizable in The Life Aquatic, Rushmore, The Royal Tenenbaums & The Darjeeling Limited.  The film also blatantly displays a socialistic viewpoint (much like Rushmore) in where the kids are the product of a sterile, systematic environment molded by a regimented scout troupe by means of uncaring, inept parents & dumbfounded adults.  Shamefully, the strong cast this film has assembled (Murray, Willis, McDormand, Norton) was wasted as character cannon fodder for the mundane, misguided love affair between two cardboard cutout kids & taking a backseat to the story's development.  The character's are all 2 dimensional & make no emotional connection to its' audience, hence the reason I wouldn't have cared if the kids jumped off that church steeple.  Much like the Life Aquatic, I had a very difficult time determining when to laugh & when to take it serious which makes it somewhat emotionally strenuous.  It draws no clear distinction in its' intentions or direction, which makes it boldly pretentious.  I did not like this film & will not recommend it to anyone.  I don't care for Wes Anderson's work beyond The Royal Tenenbaums.  He's lost his touch of quirky comedic delivery & offbeat humorous, heartwarming storytelling (The Darjeeling Limited was an exception, I did mildly enjoy it).  People are drawn to pretentiousness & traditions, so that's why fans of Wes Anderson will like this film, because it follows his latest formula & since they liked most of his previous work they will give this a good review.  Most people have a hard time looking at an individual body of work for what it is & making a clear & fair judgement, which is why it only makes sense this is getting so many rave reviews.  Sorry to burst Anderson's pretentious bubble, but this film is just not good & can hardly be called entertainment.

B005LAII80|5.0|The issue with Hollywood in the past few years is studios' fascination with rebooting anything and everything. That obsession has given us some excellent features, such as Nolan's \\"Dark Knight\\" trilogy, \\"The Three Stooges\\", and \\"Rise of the Planet of the Apes\\". However, most of what we get is middling, such as the misguided \\"The Amazing Spider-man\\", to unnecessary, such as this year's \\"Total Recall\\", to outright crap, like... well, like the rest of them. So I was a bit gun shy when I saw that Lionsgate was issuing a remake of the abhorrent Sylvester Stallone vehicle \\"Judge Dredd.\\" After some positive reviews came out, I decided to give it a shot. Thanks to that decision, I have a new film that is has been firmly added to my favorite sci-fi features of all time.<br /><br />In the future, the world is a desert wasteland, and the only life is housed in a giant city spanning multiple U.S. states, appropriately called Mega-City. Crime is out of control in Mega-City, and the only line of defense against them is the Hall of Justice. Working for the Hall are armored super-cops called Judges, who possess sweet motorcycles, tight leather chaps, and pistols that are capable of firing explosives, taser rounds, and splitter shots, to name a few. The best of these Judges is Dredd (Karl Urban), a seemingly emotionless man whose only goal in life is to deliver fair, by-the-books justice. But when he is tasked with training the unstable, psychic rookie Judge Anderson (Olivia Thirlby), his morals are put to the test. The two find themselves locked in a 200-story apartment complex, and pitted against an army of goons amped up on a dangerous drug called \\"Slo-Mo\\", all controlled by the ruthless gang boss affectionately called \\"Ma-Ma\\" (Lena Headey.) Throughout the course of one night, they must survive both their assailants and each other.<br /><br />\\"Dredd\\", in the face of so many detractors (myself included), is a triumph in both action and science fiction cinema. Its gunfights are more tense than any scene in \\"The Expendables 2\\", and the near-future setting is far more enthralling than anything we saw in \\"Prometheus.\\" The dialogue is very minimalist, and fits the overall tone of the film quite well. Dredd's one-liners never feel hammy and are always natural, thanks to Urban's surprising performance. Thirlby conveys her character's pathos and apprehension beautifully, to the point of her almost stealing the show from everybody else in the movie. Rivaling her is Headey, who is deliciously evil and obviously having a great time playing somebody with no redeeming qualities whatsoever. This is a movie that one could watch and enjoy for the acting alone.<br /><br />Action buffs have nothing to worry about, either. The shootouts are exciting and packed with some of the most visceral gore this side of a slasher flick, yet never cross into exploitation territory. More tension is packed onto these scenes due to the fact that ammo conservation and utilization of stealth are very real issues, giving more realism to the situation. The two Judges never feel superhuman, and are just as vulnerable as the droves of criminals that they pump full of lead. Special props should also be given to the slow motion effects, which are some of the best I've seen since \\"300\\", and in my opinion, raise a new standard from what we should expect from a Hollywood blockbuster. This film has less than a quarter of \\"Avatar\\"'s budget, yet its effects are far more convincing, and I daresay better, than James Cameron's $300 Million+ ego trip. Studios need to take note that it's not the size of the budget that matters, it's all in how you use it.<br /><br />Every element of this film holds up to critical scrutiny, from the acting to the dialogue to the action sequences. Even Paul Leonard-Morgan's score is enthralling. A sci-fi/action movie hasn't captured me this much since \\"The Matrix\\", or even \\"Terminator 2: Judgement Day\\", two films which I feel are on this one's level. This is oneexample of a reboot that went beautifully right, and a definite must-see for moviegoers. \\"Dredd\\" is a triumph in action cinema, in sci-fi cinema, and in film in general. One of this year's best movies, one of the best action films of the past decade, and arguably the best reboot since \\"Batman Begins.\\"<br /><br />Grade: A-example of a reboot that went beautifully right, and a definite must-see for moviegoers. \\"Dredd\\" is a triumph in action cinema, in sci-fi cinema, and in film in general. One of this year's best movies, one of the best action films of the past decade, and arguably the best reboot since \\"Batman Begins.\\"<br /><br />Grade: A-

0385341075|4.0|Bougainville. A tropical island North of Australia, between New Guinea and the Solomons. All the whites, the former employees of a big copper mine, have left except for one, the eccentric Mr. Watts who is married to one of the islanders. Mr. Watts volunteers to teach school, despite his avowed lack of knowledge on many subjects. But he is clearly a born teacher, and he is soon holding the children spellbound with daily readings from GREAT EXPECTATIONS, his sole textbook. He also has parents come in to share fragments of their own knowledge, or even to debate his resolute atheism; like a kind of educational stone soup, the curriculum soon fills out to a full meal.<br /><br />All this is narrated in retrospect by a young girl named Matilda. Her father has moved to Australia, and she lives with her religious mother who regards Mr. Watts and Pip both as emissaries of the devil. But Matilda is enraptured, entering a world of the imagination that is richer by far than anything on the island. When the book is accidentally destroyed, she joins with her classmates in trying to reconstruct its scenes from memory. Later still, she is enthralled when Mr. Watts, like a second Homer, recounts his own version, combining Dickens with his own life and the stories of the islanders. For half the book at least, this is a wondrous exploration of the magic of literature and the power of good teaching.<br /><br />But Bougainville is no Utopia. In 1990, a group of revolutionaries, incensed by the despoliation of the island by the copper company, succeeded in getting the mine closed and declared independence. New Guinea, which claimed sovereignty, imposed a blockade and sent forces to combat the rebels, with numerous atrocities on both sides. These true events are inadequately explained in the novel, but the two forces -- the local \\"Rambos\\" and the invading \\"Redskins\\" -- lurk on the periphery as a threat of unpredictable violence. Until suddenly the violence turns immediate, brutal, and horrific, and the book becomes something else entirely.<br /><br />As it happens, I have just read another novel in which childhood innocence was juxtaposed with inhuman atrocity: IN THE SHADOW OF THE BANYAN, by Vaddey Ratner, set in Cambodia under the Khmer Rouge. Both authors describe things that really happened, but Vaddey Ratner admits to writing autobiographically, while Lloyd Jones clearly is not. So I found the contrast harder to take in his case, since the book is his own construct; to my mnd, he betrays a basic contract with the reader by leading us one way and then switching to a different tone entirely. Nor can I believe that Matilda can move on with so little apparent trauma after witnessing what she does. But then I don't think that Jones really knew what kind of book he was trying to write; the last third of the book changes tack too many times, to end in a drawn-out and anticlimactic epilogue. Which is a pity, since the opening is so marvelous. B007C3TVNA|4.0|\\"We Need to Talk About Kevin\\" is full of subtleties, uncertainties, and improbabilities. We are told very little about the characters, and what we are shown is often marginal. Examples: I wanted to know more about the family's background, Kevin's condition (antisocial personality disorder or what?), how he interacts (or doesn't) with the other kids at his school, and most importantly, how he manages to commit this improbable crime. Yet, having said this I have to ask, \\"What difference would all that have made?\\" As Kevin himself states, \\"That's the point: There is no point.\\" Although the minimalism works in the end, fans of traditional film narrative may find this one a bit frustrating. Having seen it, I'm glad I did, but it's not the sort of thing I want to see again any time soon.<br /><br />The whole mood is, as you can imagine, very bleak, and, for lack of a better term, semi-surreal. The dialogue is especially important: e.g., Kevin: \\"You can be kind of harsh sometimes\\"; Eva (his mother): \\"You're a fine one to talk.\\" The physical traits Kevin and Eva share, while Kevin's father and sister share fairer traits, serve to symbolize how Eva is blamed so cruelly for Kevin's crime and the guilt she feels over same. What \\"WNTAK\\" shows and does not show leaves room for all kinds of interpretations, but I think it shows very effectively how society is cruel to everyone, and everyone learns, often in the hardest possible ways, from it.<br /><br />The first hour or so is erratic and often confusing with all the jumping back and forth in time, but everything comes into sharper focus during the second half of \\"WNTAK.\\" The fine, fine acting by Swinton, Reilly, and all three actors who play Kevin during various stages of his life is an indispensable strength here: Weaker or merely competent acting would have made this film quite forgettable.

B003P2WJ6S|5.0|How would you feel if your doctor took a sample of your tissue without your consent and then used it to further his own research studies and then shared that tissue with laboratories and medical research centers around the world for a profit?<br /><br />After your death, what would happen if your family discovered that your cells had been used in medical research against the family's wishes for years, but that those cells had also been used to make some of the most important innovations that modern science has ever known?<br /><br />This is what happened to an African American woman named Henrietta Lacks in 1951. Henrietta was diagnosed with an aggressive form of cancer that eventually led to her death. Her doctors at Johns Hopkins Hospital in Baltimore discovered that Henrietta's cells were immortal, meaning that they could be grown in a lab without dying after a few cell divisions. Samples of Henrietta's cancerous tissue were taken without her knowledge or consent during her treatments and then again after her death. Henrietta's cells were used to conduct many experiments from developing a cure for polio and studying the effects of radiation and other toxic substances on the human body to gene mapping as well as cancer and AIDS research. Her cells represented a boon to medical research.<br /><br />In the meantime, Henrietta's family had been kept in the dark. They lived in poverty and poor health, often without health insurance, and they only found out about her contribution to science years later.<br /><br />Journalist Rebecca Skloot does an excellent job of gathering what little information there is on Henrietta's life and telling her remarkable story and the aftermath of her death. It's unusual for a journalist to get so close to the subjects she is writing about, but Rebecca's close relationship with the family only made me feel more indignant about how they were treated. The end of the novel, in particular, was a real eye-opener for me. I had spent so much time feeling angry about what had happened to the Lacks' that I never stopped to consider what would've happened if Henrietta's cells hadn't been used.<br /><br />This isn't a story about Henrietta Lacks, although she is certainly the most important figure in this novel. It's about her children, her friends, and the debate over whether or not human tissue should be used with or without a patient's consent and what that means for the future of medical science.

0316228532|4.0|Another review here from someone who has actually read the whole book. And I do feel like I should offer some sort of disclaimer here- I am a huge Harry Potter fan and grew up with the books. However, Harry Potter has not blinkered my reading career, as much as I loved the books, I love other books more and am currently doing my literature degree at university. I feel like this is important to mention because whilst J.K Rowling's name drew me to the book in the first place, it is not what made me read it and not what made me enjoy it.<br /><br />Truthfully, I think the publication of The Casual Vacancy is rather brave, because this is a book that will polarize readers. It is absolutely nothing like Harry Potter and it is absolutely not for anyone under the age of 15, minimum. Readers wanting puppies, kittens and magic spells should really look elsewhere because this is grim, stark reality. It reminded me far more of Christos Tsiolkas' award winning The Slap than anything else, both books have catalyst events that pave the way for the action of the novel and changing lives of cast of characters, who share the role of protagonist. Also like The Slap, and like much of the work of many great writers, The Casual Vacancy is a form of social inquiry. Rowling is deftly weaving her story around societal issues, problems of class, politics, family, small town life and, at its very heart, human nature.<br /><br />For the first part of the book I found the rapidly changing perspectives rather confusing, simply because there were so many characters to keep track of, I found myself mentally going through a roster in order to work out exactly who was who. Of course, this did not last and by the end of the novel each character had a distinctive voice, personality and story arch that I remembered and enjoyed. At times it also verges on preachy as Rowling shows us SO many problems at once and her voice breaks through the story which is distracting and annoying, but it doesn't happenoften.<br /><br />I found it a fast read, I couldn't put it down and finished in under a day, and thoroughly enjoyed every minute of it. The book is gritty and ultimately tragic but it is also full of humour and whilst Rowling reveals her characters' abundant flaws, they are also sympathetic and relatable. They come across as real people trying to survive and live, warts and all. From smug, aspirational and distinctly Dursley-like Howard and Shirley Mollison, to humorless Kay and rebellious Fats ... these are people you will recognize. There is no immediate hero to latch onto (though my favourites were Sukhvinda, Krystal and- surprisingly- Samantha) and the reader is forced to understand the characters as three dimensional, flawed, real people.<br /><br />This book made me cry because it is real, the ultimate problem of the book is, as the author said `What do we do about Krystal?' the ultimate tragedy of the book is that embroiled in politics, small town life, self consciousness and hubris- no one does enough.  This is an honest book and it's a big issue book. It has a lot to say about the problems of our affluent societies and the human condition.<br /><br />I take my hat off to you J.K Rowling because this was an excellent book. It's not Harry Potter, it's a literary novel. People are either going to love it or hate it. It's going to get a lot of negative reviews. It's not going to change the world, it's not a masterpiece. But I thoroughly enjoyed it. It made me feel and it made me think, and that is all I can ask for.

B0083TXXZ4|5.0|We own a couple of nerf guns that my children (ages 7, 5, and 3) like to play with, but nothing over six shots.  So it's a bit of an understatement when I say they were excited to see this toy, which comes with enough hardware (well, plasticware :-) ) to shoot 24 shots once assembled.<br /><br />Assembly was fairly simple, although the instructions could have been a little clearer.  You install four batteries, snap a couple of plastic parts to the main assembly, load up and insert the 4 supplied clips, and you're ready to go.  The only tricky part is installing the plastic parts ...you need to press a couple of pieces firmly in a way that isn't really illustrated by the instructions.  But its not too difficult to figure out, and once you do, everything fits together snugly and correctly.<br /><br />The gun comes equipped with four clips/magazines, and each clip can hold six darts.  The gun itself can hold up to eight clips, and optional high capacity clips can hold up to 18 nerf darts each.  If you want to be able to shoot more darts and if your intent is to get into nerf battles with teenagers/young adults, by all means get the high capacity clips, otherwise you can buy four more clips and 24 more darts for about 15 dollars. To fire the gun, you have to hold one trigger that basically powers up the gun and pull another trigger to fire the gun.  The triggers are above/below each other, so you do it with one hand.  Once you shoot a clip empty, there is a handle on top of the gun that you pull to rotate the next clip into position.<br /><br />For its price, the nerf gun works well.  The box states that the darts will go up to 75 feet, and I find that to be pretty accurate if you angle the gun up a bit. You are not going to get reliable accuracy much past 15 or 20 feet, because the darts don't have any thing to stabilize themselves in-flight, but you can really shoot quickly, so what you lack in accuracy you can make up in volume of fire.  The \\"elite\\" nerf darts seem a little stiffer than the darts we already own, and I suspect this is to work a little better in the clips and to fly a little better.  Because the gun shoots so quickly, your children will spend much more time picking up the darts and reloading the clips into the gun than they will shooting.  However, I'm fine with that as it keeps them occupied.  :-)<br /><br />All three of my kids love this toy.  Although the gun is a little heavy and bulky, my seven year old can handle it well. My five year old girl can hold the gun, but has a little trouble working both triggers ... the trigger that shoots a dart takes a little bit strength to pull, so she needs to use a couple of fingers, and isn't able to maintain pressure on the trigger that powers the gun.  If there is a flaw in the gun, it's that there ought to be a way to set the gun so that you don't need to hold the lower trigger, short of taping it down.  I believe the reason they made it this way was to both enhance battery life - you only use power when you're holding the lower trigger - and perhaps so the gun is silent when you're hiding and waiting to ambush someone else. But it would be nice if there was a way to override that trigger.  Finally, my three year old was too small to do much other than pull the trigger to shoot while I held the gun for him, but he loved the gun, too.<br /><br />Overall, this is a fun toy for your kids, or even for someone older who likes nerf toys.  While it comes equipped well enough to play, I've already ordered more 6-shot clips and darts at the request of my kids, both to be able to shoot more shots at a time and to replace some lost darts. And consult with your pets before you buy this, as this toy has the potential to really stress them out, especially if you trick out the gun so your little ones could fire 144 darts in the general direction of your furry friends whenever you're not nearby.  :-)  Five stars.little stiffer than the darts we already own, and I suspect this is to work a little better in the clips and to fly a little better.
